CentralNotice --></div>\n\t<div class="mw-indicators mw-body-content">\n\t</div>\n\t<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" >Boeing 737 MAX</h1>\n\t<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">\n\t\t<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>\n\t\t<div id="contentSub"></div>\n\t\t<div id="contentSub2"></div>\n\t\t\n\t\t<div dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Airliner family by Boeing</div>\n<p class="mw-empty-elt">\n\n</p>\n<table class="infobox" style="width:25.5em;border-spacing:2px;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th colspan="2" style="text-align: center; font-size: large; padding-bottom: 0.3em;">Boeing 737 MAX\n</th></tr>\n\n\n<tr>\n<td colspan="2" style="text-align: center;"><a href="/wiki/File:Boeing_737-8_MAX_N8704Q_rotated.jpg" class="image" title="Boeing 737-8 MAX N8704Q at /></a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="2" style="border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;text-align:center;">The 737 MAX is a fourth-generation Boeing 737, re-engined with CFM LEAP-1B turbofans.\n</td></tr>\n\n<tr>\n<th>Role\n</th>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Narrow-body" class="mw-redirect" title="Narrow-body">Narrow-body</a> <a href="/wiki/Jet_airliner" title="Jet airliner">jet airliner</a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th><span class="nowrap">National origin</span>\n</th>\n<td>United States\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th>Manufacturer\n</th>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Boeing_Commercial_Airplanes" title="Boeing Commercial Airplanes">Boeing Commercial Airplanes</a>\n</td></tr>\n\n\n\n<tr>\n<th>First flight\n</th>\n<td>January 29, 2016<sup id="cite_ref-ST160129_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ST160129-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n\n<tr>\n<th>Introduction\n</th>\n<td>May 22, 2017 with <a href="/wiki/Malindo_Air" title="Malindo Air">Malindo Air</a><sup id="cite_ref-Flight22May2017_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flight22May2017-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n\n<tr>\n<th>Status\n</th>\n<td>In service\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th>Primary&#160;users\n</th>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Southwest_Airlines" title="Southwest Airlines">Southwest Airlines</a><br /><a href="/wiki/American_Airlines" Southern Airlines</a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th>Produced\n</th>\n<td>2014<sup id="cite_ref-Production_begins_737MAX_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Production_begins_737MAX-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>\xe2\x80\x93present<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;a&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th><span class="nowrap">Number built</span>\n</th>\n<td>472 delivered as of 31&#160;March&#160;2021<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737_MAX&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-O_D_summ_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-O_D_summ-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th><span class="nowrap">Developed from</span>\n</th>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Boeing_737_Next_Generation" title="Boeing 737 Next Generation">Boeing 737 Next Generation</a>\n</td></tr>\n\n\n</tbody></table>\n<p>The <b>Boeing 737 MAX</b> is the fourth generation of <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737" title="Boeing 737">Boeing 737</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Narrow-body_aircraft" title="Narrow-body aircraft">narrow-body airliner</a> manufactured by <a href="/wiki/Boeing_Commercial_Airplanes" title="Boeing Commercial Airplanes">Boeing Commercial Airplanes</a> (BCA). It succeeds the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737_Next_Generation" title="Boeing 737 Next Generation">Boeing 737 Next Generation</a> (NG) and competes with the <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A320neo_family" title="Airbus A320neo family">Airbus A320neo family</a>.\nThe 737 MAX is based on earlier 737 designs, with more efficient <a href="/wiki/CFM_International_LEAP" title="CFM International LEAP">CFM International LEAP</a>-1B engines, aerodynamic changes, including its distinctive split-tip <a href="/wiki/Winglet" class="mw-redirect" title="Winglet">winglets</a>, and airframe modifications.\nThe new series was publicly announced on August 30, 2011. It took its maiden flight on January 29, 2016 and was certified by the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Federal_Aviation_Administration" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Federal Aviation Administration">U.S. Federal Aviation Administration</a> (FAA) in March 2017. The first delivery was a MAX&#160;8 in May 2017 to <a href="/wiki/Malindo_Air" title="Malindo Air">Malindo Air</a>, with whom it commenced service on May 22, 2017.\n</p><p>The 737 MAX series has been offered in four variants, offering 138 to 204 seats in typical two-class configuration<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> and a 3,215 to 3,825&#160;nmi (5,954 to 7,084&#160;km) range. The 737 MAX&#160;7, MAX&#160;8 (including the denser, 200\xe2\x80\x93seat MAX 200), and MAX&#160;9 are intended to replace the 737-700, -800, and -900 respectively, and a further-stretched 737 MAX&#160;10. As of November 2020, the Boeing 737 MAX had a backlog of 3,290 units.<sup id="cite_ref-Boeing_O&amp;D_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boeing_O&amp;D-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The 737 MAX suffered a recurring failure in the <a href="/wiki/Maneuvering_Characteristics_Augmentation_System" title="Maneuvering Characteristics Augmentation System">Maneuvering Characteristics Augmentation System</a> (MCAS) causing two fatal accidents, <a href="/wiki/Lion_Air_Flight_610" title="Lion Air Flight 610">Lion Air Flight 610</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Airlines_Flight_302" title="Ethiopian Airlines Flight 302">Ethiopian Airlines Flight 302</a>, in which 346 people died.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> It was subsequently <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737_MAX_groundings" title="Boeing 737 MAX groundings">grounded worldwide</a> from March 2019 to November 2020. Investigations faulted a <a href="/wiki/Cover-up" title="Cover-up">cover-up</a> of a known defect by Boeing, and lapses in the certification by the FAA.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> After being charged with <a href="/wiki/Fraud" title="Fraud">fraud</a>, Boeing <a href="/wiki/Settlement_(litigation)" title="Settlement (litigation)">settled</a> to pay over $2.5 billion.\n</p><p>When production temporarily halted in January 2020, Boeing had over 400 finished but undeliverable aircraft. Production resumed in May 2020 at a low rate. On November 18, 2020, the FAA cleared the MAX to resume service, subject to a list of mandated design and training changes. Transport Canada and EASA both cleared the MAX in late January 2021, subject to additional requirements. \nAs of March&#160;2021<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737_MAX&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, a total of 4,437 aircraft had been ordered of which 472 had been delivered.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc" role="navigation" aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-29"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Development">Development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737_MAX&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737_MAX&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In 2006, Boeing started considering the replacement of the 737 with a "clean-sheet" design that could follow the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_787_Dreamliner" title="Boeing 787 Dreamliner">Boeing 787 Dreamliner</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> In June 2010, a decision on this replacement was postponed into 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On December 1, 2010, Boeing\'s competitor, <a href="/wiki/Airbus" title="Airbus">Airbus</a>, launched the <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A320neo_family" title="Airbus A320neo family">Airbus A320neo family</a> to improve <a href="/wiki/Fuel_economy_in_aircraft" title="Fuel economy in aircraft">fuel burn</a> and operating efficiency with new engines: the <a href="/wiki/CFM_International" title="CFM International">CFM International</a> <a href="/wiki/CFM_International_LEAP" title="CFM International LEAP">LEAP</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Pratt_%26_Whitney" title="Pratt &amp; Whitney">Pratt &amp; Whitney</a> <a href="/wiki/Pratt_%26_Whitney_PW1000G" title="Pratt &amp; Whitney PW1000G">PW1000G</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> In February 2011, Boeing\'s CEO <a href="/wiki/Jim_McNerney" class="mw-redirect" title="Jim McNerney">Jim McNerney</a> maintained "We\'re going to do a new airplane."<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> In March 2011, BCA President <a href="/wiki/James_Albaugh" title="James Albaugh">James Albaugh</a> told participants of a trade meeting the company was not sure about a 737 re-engine, like Boeing CFO <a href="/wiki/James_A._Bell" title="James A. Bell">James A. Bell</a> stated at an investor conference the same month.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> The Airbus A320neo gathered 667 commitments at the June, 2011 <a href="/wiki/Paris_Air_Show" title="Paris Air Show">Paris Air Show</a> for a backlog of 1,029 units since its launch, setting an order record for a new commercial airliner.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On July 20, 2011, <a href="/wiki/American_Airlines" title="American Airlines">American Airlines</a> announced an order for 460 narrowbody jets including 130 <a href="/wiki/A320ceo" class="mw-redirect" title="A320ceo">A320ceos</a> (Current Engine Option) and 130 A320neos, and intended to order 100 <a href="/wiki/Re-engine" class="mw-redirect" title="Re-engine">re-engined</a> 737s with CFM LEAPs, pending Boeing confirmation.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> The order broke Boeing\'s monopoly with the airline and forced Boeing into a re-engined 737.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> As this sale included a <a href="/wiki/Most-Favoured-Customer_Clause" title="Most-Favoured-Customer Clause">Most-Favoured-Customer Clause</a>, Airbus has to refund any difference to American if it sells to another airline at a lower price, so the European manufacturer was unable to offer it at a price which <a href="/wiki/United_Airlines" title="United Airlines">United Airlines</a> deems "competitive" leaving it with a Boeing-skewed fleet.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Program_launch">Program launch</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737_MAX&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Program launch">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:737MAX_ILA_2012.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/737MAX_ILA_2012.png/280px-737MAX_ILA_2012.png" decoding="async" width="280" height="85" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/737MAX_ILA_2012.png/420px-737MAX_ILA_2012.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/737MAX_ILA_2012.png/560px-737MAX_ILA_2012.png 2x" data-file-width="1290" data-file-height="393" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:737MAX_ILA_2012.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 737 MAX 9 mockup at the 2012 ILA Berlin</div></div></div>\n<p>On August 30, 2011, Boeing\'s board of directors approved the launch of the re-engined 737, expecting a 4% lower fuel burn than the Airbus A320neo.<sup id="cite_ref-Boeing30aug2011_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boeing30aug2011-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Studies for additional drag reduction were performed during 2011, including revised tail cone, natural laminar flow nacelle, and hybrid laminar flow vertical stabilizer.<sup id="cite_ref-FG-20110830_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FG-20110830-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Boeing abandoned the development of a new design.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Boeing expected the 737 MAX to meet or exceed the range of the Airbus A320neo.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Firm configuration for the 737 MAX was scheduled for 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In March 2010, the estimated cost to re-engine the 737, according to Mike Bair, Boeing Commercial Airplanes\' vice president of business strategy and marketing, would be US$2\xe2\x80\x933&#160;billion, including the CFM engine development. During Boeing\'s Q2 2011 earnings call, former CFO James Bell said the development cost for the airframe only would be 10\xe2\x80\x9315% of the cost of a new program estimated at US$10\xe2\x80\x9312&#160;billion at the time. <a href="/wiki/Bernstein_Research" class="mw-redirect" title="Bernstein Research">Bernstein Research</a> predicted in January 2012, that this cost would be twice that of the A320neo.<sup id="cite_ref-FG27jan2012_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FG27jan2012-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> The MAX development cost could have been well over the internal target of US$2&#160;bn, and closer to US$4&#160;bn.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Fuel consumption is reduced by 14% from the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737_Next_Generation" title="Boeing 737 Next Generation">737NG</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-AW12May2017_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AW12May2017-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Southwest_Airlines" title="Southwest Airlines">Southwest Airlines</a> was signed up as the launch customer in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In November 2014, McNerney said the 737 would be replaced by a new airplane by 2030\xe2\x80\x94probably using composite materials\xe2\x80\x94that would be slightly bigger and have new engines, but would retain the 737\'s general configuration.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737_MAX&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Boeing_737_MAX_(23514088802).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Boeing_737_MAX_%2823514088802%29.jpg/220px-Boeing_737_MAX_%2823514088802%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Boeing_737_MAX_%2823514088802%29.jpg/330px-Boeing_737_MAX_%2823514088802%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Boeing_737_MAX_%2823514088802%29.jpg/440px-Boeing_737_MAX_%2823514088802%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Boeing_737_MAX_(23514088802).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Roll-out of the first Boeing 737 MAX in December 2015</div></div></div>\n<p>On August 13, 2015, the first 737 MAX fuselage completed assembly at <a href="/wiki/Spirit_Aerosystems" class="mw-redirect" title="Spirit Aerosystems">Spirit Aerosystems</a> in <a href="/wiki/Wichita,_Kansas" title="Wichita, Kansas">Wichita, Kansas</a>, for a test aircraft that would eventually be delivered to launch customer Southwest Airlines.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> On December 8, 2015, the first 737 MAX\xe2\x80\x94a MAX&#160;8 named <i>Spirit of Renton</i>\xe2\x80\x94was rolled out at the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_Renton_Factory" title="Boeing Renton Factory">Boeing Renton Factory</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Because <a href="/wiki/GKN" title="GKN">GKN</a> could not produce the <a href="/wiki/Titanium" title="Titanium">titanium</a> <a href="/wiki/Honeycomb_structure" title="Honeycomb structure">honeycomb</a> inner walls for the <a href="/wiki/Thrust_reverser" class="mw-redirect" title="Thrust reverser">thrust reversers</a> quickly enough, Boeing switched to a composite part produced by Spirit to deliver 47 MAXs per month in 2017. Spirit supplies 69% of the 737 <a href="/wiki/Airframe" title="Airframe">airframe</a>, including the fuselage, thrust reverser, engine <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_pylon" class="mw-redirect" title="Aircraft pylon">pylons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nacelle" title="Nacelle">nacelles</a>, and wing <a href="/wiki/Leading_edge" title="Leading edge">leading edges</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A new spar-assembly line with <a href="/wiki/Industrial_robot" title="Industrial robot">robotic</a> drilling machines should increase throughput by 33%. The Electroimpact automated panel assembly line sped up the wing lower-skin assembly by 35%.<sup id="cite_ref-AW170215_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AW170215-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Boeing planned to increase its 737 MAX monthly production rate from 42 planes in 2017, to 57 planes by 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> The new spar-assembly line is designed by Electroimpact.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Electroimpact has also installed fully automated riveting machines and tooling to fasten stringers to the wing skin.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The rate increase strained the production and by August 2018, over 40 unfinished jets were parked in Renton, awaiting parts or engine installation, as CFM engines and Spirit fuselages were delivered late.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> After parked airplanes peaked at 53 at the beginning of September, Boeing reduced this by nine the following month, as deliveries rose to 61 from 29 in July and 48 in August.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On September 23, 2015, Boeing announced a collaboration with <a href="/wiki/Comac" title="Comac">Comac</a> to build a completion and delivery facility for the 737,<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> in <a href="/wiki/Zhoushan" title="Zhoushan">Zhoushan</a>, China,<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> the first outside the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> This facility initially handles interior finishing only, but will subsequently be expanded to include paintwork. The first aircraft was delivered from the facility to Air China on December 15, 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The largest part of the suppliers cost are the <a href="/wiki/Aerostructures" class="mw-redirect" title="Aerostructures">aerostructures</a> with US$10\xe2\x80\x9312M (35-34% of the US$ 28.5-35 M total), followed by the engines with US$7\xe2\x80\x939M (25-26%), systems and interiors with US$5\xe2\x80\x936M each (18-17%), then <a href="/wiki/Avionics" title="Avionics">avionics</a> with US$1.5\xe2\x80\x932M (5-6%).<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flight_testing_and_certification">Flight testing and certification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737_MAX&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Flight testing and certification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737_MAX_certification" title="Boeing 737 MAX certification">Boeing 737 MAX certification</a></div>\n<p>The first flight took place on January 29, 2016, at <a href="/wiki/Renton_Municipal_Airport" title="Renton Municipal Airport">Renton Municipal Airport</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-seattletimes20190317_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-seattletimes20190317-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> nearly 49 years after the maiden flight of the original 737-100, on April 9, 1967.<sup id="cite_ref-ST160129_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ST160129-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> The first MAX 8, 1A001, was used for aerodynamic trials: flutter testing, stability and control, and takeoff performance-data verification, before it was modified for an operator and delivered. 1A002 was used for performance and engine testing: climb and landing performance, crosswind, noise, cold weather, high altitude, <a href="/wiki/Fuel_economy_in_aircraft" title="Fuel economy in aircraft">fuel burn</a> and water-ingestion. Aircraft systems including autoland were tested with 1A003. 1A004, with an airliner layout, flew function-and-reliability certification for 300h with a light flight-test instrumentation.<sup id="cite_ref-AIN8Nov2017_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AIN8Nov2017-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The 737 MAX gained <a href="/wiki/Federal_Aviation_Administration" title="Federal Aviation Administration">Federal Aviation Administration</a> (FAA) certification on March 8, 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-FAA-TCDS_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAA-TCDS-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Boeing9March2017_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boeing9March2017-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> It was approved by EASA on March 27, 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> After completing 2,000 test flight hours and 180-minute <a href="/wiki/ETOPS" title="ETOPS">ETOPS</a> testing requiring 3,000 simulated flight cycles in April 2017, CFM International notified Boeing of a possible manufacturing quality issue with low pressure <a href="/wiki/Turbine" title="Turbine">turbine</a> (LPT) discs in LEAP-1B engines.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Boeing suspended 737 MAX flights on May 4,<sup id="cite_ref-Flight16may2017_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flight16may2017-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> and resumed flights on May 12.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the certification process, the FAA delegated many evaluations to Boeing, allowing the manufacturer to review their own product.<sup id="cite_ref-seattletimes20190317_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-seattletimes20190317-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> It was widely reported that Boeing pushed to expedite approval of the 737 MAX to compete with the Airbus A320neo, which hit the market nine months ahead of Boeing\'s model.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Introduction">Introduction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737_MAX&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Introduction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:9M-LRD_(37495597164).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/9M-LRD_%2837495597164%29.jpg/220px-9M-LRD_%2837495597164%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="92" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/9M-LRD_%2837495597164%29.jpg/330px-9M-LRD_%2837495597164%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/9M-LRD_%2837495597164%29.jpg/440px-9M-LRD_%2837495597164%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2801" data-file-height="1168" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:9M-LRD_(37495597164).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Boeing 737 MAX 8 entered service with <a href="/wiki/Lion_Air" title="Lion Air">Lion Air</a>\'s subsidiary <a href="/wiki/Malindo_Air" title="Malindo Air">Malindo Air</a> (wearing Batik Air Malaysia livery)</div></div></div>\n<p>The first delivery was a MAX&#160;8, handed over to <a href="/wiki/Malindo_Air" title="Malindo Air">Malindo Air</a> (a subsidiary of <a href="/wiki/Lion_Air" title="Lion Air">Lion Air</a>) on May 16, 2017; it entered service on May 22.<sup id="cite_ref-Flight22May2017_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flight22May2017-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Air_International" title="Norwegian Air International">Norwegian Air International</a> was the second airline to put a 737 MAX into service, when it performed its first <a href="/wiki/Transatlantic_flight" title="Transatlantic flight">transatlantic flight</a> with a MAX&#160;8 named <i><a href="/wiki/Freddie_Laker" title="Freddie Laker">Sir Freddie Laker</a></i> on July 15, 2017, between <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh_Airport" title="Edinburgh Airport">Edinburgh Airport</a> in Scotland and <a href="/wiki/Bradley_International_Airport" title="Bradley International Airport">Bradley International Airport</a> in the U.S. state of Connecticut.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Boeing aimed to match the 99.7% dispatch reliability of the 737 Next Generation (NG).<sup id="cite_ref-Boeing_optimistic_for_early_737_Max_dispatch_reliability_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boeing_optimistic_for_early_737_Max_dispatch_reliability-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Southwest Airlines, the launch customer, took delivery of its first <span class="nowrap">737 MAX</span> on August 29, 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Boeing planned to deliver at least 50 to 75 aircraft in 2017, 10\xe2\x80\x9315% of the more than five hundred 737s to be delivered in the year.<sup id="cite_ref-Flight16may2017_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flight16may2017-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After one year of service, 130 MAXs had been delivered to 28 customers, logging over 41,000<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>flights in 118,000<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>hours and flying over 6.5<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>million passengers. <a href="/wiki/Flydubai" title="Flydubai">flydubai</a> observed 15% more efficiency than the NG, more than the 14% promised, and <a href="/wiki/Dependability" title="Dependability">dependability</a> reached 99.4%. Long routes include 24 over 2,500&#160;nmi (4,630&#160;km), including a daily <a href="/wiki/Aerolineas_Argentinas" class="mw-redirect" title="Aerolineas Argentinas">Aerolineas Argentinas</a> service from <a href="/wiki/Buenos_Aires-Ezeiza" class="mw-redirect" title="Buenos Aires-Ezeiza">Buenos Aires</a> to <a href="/wiki/Punta_Cana_Airport" class="mw-redirect" title="Punta Cana Airport">Punta Cana</a> over 3,252&#160;nmi (6,023&#160;km).<sup id="cite_ref-Tinseth22May2018_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tinseth22May2018-60">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2019, <a href="/wiki/Moody%27s" class="mw-redirect" title="Moody&#39;s">Moody\'s</a> had estimated Boeing\'s <a href="/wiki/Operating_margin" title="Operating margin">operating margin</a> to be US$12\xe2\x80\x9315 million for each 737 Max 8 at its list price of $121.6 million, although the list price is usually discounted 50-55% in practice. This high margin was made possible by the efficiencies of production volume and the amortization of development costs and capital investment over the decades of the program run. However, costs have since risen significantly and the margin reduced following the second crash, the FAA grounding, and the severe disruption to production.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-IBD140319_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IBD140319-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> \nBoeing estimated it would cost an additional $6.3 billion to produce the remaining 737 MAX program, $4 billion for "future abnormal costs" as production restarted, plus an estimated $8.3 billion for concessions and compensation to customers.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-boeing_Jul_18_2019_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boeing_Jul_18_2019-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> The rising costs also led Moody\'s to downgrade Boeing\'s <a href="/wiki/Bond_credit_rating" title="Bond credit rating">credit rating</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Moody&#39;s_cut_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moody&#39;s_cut-66">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Grounding_and_recertification">Grounding and recertification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737_MAX&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Grounding and recertification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737_MAX_groundings" title="Boeing 737 MAX groundings">Boeing 737 MAX groundings</a></div>\n<p>The Boeing 737 MAX was <a href="/wiki/List_of_Boeing_737_MAX_groundings" title="List of Boeing 737 MAX groundings">grounded</a> worldwide from March 2019 to November 2020, after a malfunctioning flight control system caused two new aircraft to crash in Indonesia and Ethiopia between October 2018 and March 2019, killing all 346 people on board. In the following eighteen months, Boeing redesigned the computer architecture that supported <a href="/wiki/Maneuvering_Characteristics_Augmentation_System" title="Maneuvering Characteristics Augmentation System">MCAS</a>, while investigations faulted aircraft design and <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737_MAX_certification" title="Boeing 737 MAX certification">certification</a> lapses. Boeing faces legal and <a href="/wiki/Financial_impact_of_the_Boeing_737_MAX_groundings" title="Financial impact of the Boeing 737 MAX groundings">financial consequences</a>, as no <a href="/wiki/List_of_Boeing_737_MAX_orders_and_deliveries" title="List of Boeing 737 MAX orders and deliveries">deliveries of the MAX</a> could be made while the aircraft was grounded, and airlines canceled more orders than Boeing produced during this period; a portion of those aircraft have lost their original buyers. Boeing found <a href="/wiki/Foreign_object_damage" title="Foreign object damage">foreign object debris</a> in the fuel tanks of 35 of 50 grounded 737 MAX aircraft that were inspected, and is to check the remainder of the 400 undelivered planes.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Boeing had similar issues with 787s produced in South Carolina.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> The FAA curtailed Boeing\'s <a href="/wiki/Organization_Designation_Authorization" title="Organization Designation Authorization">delegated authority</a>, and invited global aviation stakeholders to comment on pending changes to the aircraft and to pilot training. The FAA grounding order was lifted on November 18, 2020;<sup id="cite_ref-SeattleTimesUngrounding_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SeattleTimesUngrounding-69">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> all aircraft must be repaired to comply with various <a href="/wiki/Airworthiness_directive" class="mw-redirect" title="Airworthiness directive">airworthiness directives</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After being charged with <a href="/wiki/Fraud" title="Fraud">fraud</a>, Boeing <a href="/wiki/Settlement_(litigation)" title="Settlement (litigation)">settled</a> to pay over $2.5 billion: a <a href="/wiki/Fine_(penalty)" title="Fine (penalty)">criminal monetary penalty</a> of $243.6 million, $1.77 billion of <a href="/wiki/Damages" title="Damages">damages</a> to airline customers, and a $500 million crash-victim beneficiaries fund.<sup id="cite_ref-USDOJ7jan2021_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USDOJ7jan2021-71">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Production_slowdown_and_suspension">Production slowdown and suspension</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737_MAX&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Production slowdown and suspension">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>From mid-April 2019, the company announced that it was temporarily cutting production of the 737 aircraft from 52 per month to 42 amid the 737 MAX groundings.<sup id="cite_ref-Boeing-20190405_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boeing-20190405-73">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Production of the LEAP-1B engine continued at an unchanged rate, enabling CFM to catch up its backlog within a few weeks.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>As the 737 MAX re-certification moved into 2020, Boeing suspended production from January to conserve cash and prioritize stored aircraft delivery.<sup id="cite_ref-Boeing16dec2019_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boeing16dec2019-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-cnn-2020-01-21_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cnn-2020-01-21-76">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> The 737 MAX program was Boeing\'s largest source of profit.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Around 80% of the 737 production costs involve payments to parts suppliers, which may be as low as US$10 million per plane.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After the announcement, Moody\'s cut Boeing\'s <a href="/wiki/Bond_credit_rating" title="Bond credit rating">debt ratings</a> in December 2019, citing the rising costs due to the grounding and the production halt including financial support to suppliers and compensation to airlines and lessors which could lower the program\'s margins and cash generation for years.<sup id="cite_ref-Moody&#39;s_cut_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moody&#39;s_cut-66">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Moody\'s also warned that the production halt would have wide and harmful impact to the whole aerospace and defense supply chain and, if and when production resumes, the ramp-up would be slower than previously anticipated, as suppliers have to make adjustments to cost structures built for planned record output on the 737 program.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/CFM_International" title="CFM International">CFM International</a> reduced production of the LEAP-1B for the 737 MAX, in favor of the LEAP-1A for the Airbus A320neo, but is prepared to meet demand for both aircraft.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Boeing did not publicly say how long the suspension would last. The last pre-suspension fuselages entered final assembly in early January 2020. Boeing was reported to internally expect production to be halted for at least 60 days.<sup id="cite_ref-leeham20200107_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-leeham20200107-81">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Industry observers began to question if Boeing\'s projection of record production rate of 57 per month would ever be reached.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> In early January 2020, an issue was discovered in the software update, further delaying the return to service.<sup id="cite_ref-Reuters17Jan2020_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters17Jan2020-83">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In late January, production was expected to restart in April and take a year and a half to clear the inventory of 400 airplanes, ramping up slowly and building over time: Boeing might have delivered 180 stored jets by year-end and produce an equal number.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Boeing did not disclose any possible effect on deliveries caused by the FAA\'s withdrawal of Boeing\'s delegated authority to <a href="/wiki/Airworthiness_certificate" title="Airworthiness certificate">certify the airworthiness</a> of each aircraft.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> MAX supplier Spirit AeroSystems said it does not expect to return production rate to 52 per month until late 2022.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> By early April, the <a href="/wiki/COVID-19_pandemic" title="COVID-19 pandemic">COVID-19 pandemic</a> led Boeing to shut down its other airliner production lines<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> and further delayed recertification of the MAX.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> By late April 2020, Boeing signaled that it hoped to win regulatory approval by August 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> On May 27, 2020, Boeing resumed 737 MAX production at a low production rate, which it expected to be ramped up according to customer deliveries, towards 31 monthly in 2021.<sup id="cite_ref-Flight27may2020_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flight27may2020-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Recertification_and_delivery_plan">Recertification and delivery plan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737_MAX&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Recertification and delivery plan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Between June 29 and July 1, 2020, the FAA and Boeing conducted a series of recertification test flights.<sup id="cite_ref-FAAflighttestscompleted_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAAflighttestscompleted-90">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Transport_Canada" title="Transport Canada">Transport Canada</a> and <a href="/wiki/European_Aviation_Safety_Agency" title="European Aviation Safety Agency">EASA</a> each concluded their own independent recertification flights in late August and early September 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On August 19, 2020, Boeing announced that it had received new orders for the 737 MAX for the first time in 2020. Per a statement from the company, Poland\'s <a href="/wiki/Enter_Air" title="Enter Air">Enter Air SA</a> entered into an agreement to buy up to four 737s. <a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a> reported that Boeing referred to the airplane as a Boeing 737-8 in a move away from Boeing 737 MAX branding.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>On October 28, 2020, Boeing indicated that it expected to deliver about half of the 450 stockpiled aircraft in 2021, and the majority of the remainder in 2022, noting that some of these aircraft will need to be re-marketed and potentially reconfigured. The delivery rate will also condition the production rate for new aircraft, to avoid compounding the problem.<sup id="cite_ref-flight20201028_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-flight20201028-93">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On November 18, 2020, the FAA announced that the MAX had been cleared to return to service. Before the aircraft can resume service, repairs must be implemented per a forthcoming <a href="/wiki/Airworthiness_Directive" title="Airworthiness Directive">airworthiness directive</a> from the FAA. Airline training programs will also require approval. Passenger flights are expected to resume in the U.S. by the end of the year; deliveries can resume and production is expected to ramp up slowly.<sup id="cite_ref-SeattleTimesUngrounding_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SeattleTimesUngrounding-69">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Boeing has seen more than 1,000 order cancelations since the grounding in March 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Some of these already built aircraft have seen their order canceled and Boeing is working to find new customers to take delivery.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On December 3, 2020, American Airlines made a demonstration flight for journalists to explain the FAA-required modifications, to regain public trust.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> \nThe first airline to resume regular passenger service was Brazilian low-cost <a href="/wiki/Gol_Linhas_A%C3%A9reas_Inteligentes" title="Gol Linhas A\xc3\xa9reas Inteligentes">Gol</a>, on December 9, 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> The first in the United States was American Airlines on December 29.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Transport Canada and EASA both cleared the MAX in late January 2021, subject to additional requirements.<sup id="cite_ref-tcungrounding_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tcungrounding-99">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EASAungrounding_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EASAungrounding-100">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> Several more regulators worldwide have ungrounded the aircraft since then, including those in the <a href="/wiki/General_Civil_Aviation_Authority" title="General Civil Aviation Authority">UAE</a>, <a href="/wiki/Civil_Aviation_Safety_Authority" title="Civil Aviation Safety Authority">Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kenya_Civil_Aviation_Authority" title="Kenya Civil Aviation Authority">Kenya</a>, and <a href="/wiki/National_Civil_Aviation_Agency_of_Brazil" title="National Civil Aviation Agency of Brazil">Brazil</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Design">Design</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737_MAX&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Design">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>In mid-2011, one design objective was matching fuel burn of the 737 MAX to that of the Airbus A320neo\'s 15% fuel-burn advantage. The initial 737 Max reduction was 10\xe2\x80\x9312%; it was later enhanced to 14.5%. The fan was widened from 61&#160;in (150&#160;cm) to 69.4&#160;in (176&#160;cm) by raising the nose gear and placing the engine higher and forward. The split winglet added 1\xe2\x80\x931.5% fuel burn reduction, a re-lofted tail cone added an additional 1% reduction. Electronically controlling the <a href="/wiki/Bleed_air" title="Bleed air">bleed air</a> system improved efficiency. The new engine casings included <a href="/wiki/Turbofan#Noise" title="Turbofan">chevrons</a>, similar to those of the Boeing 787, which also helped to reduce engine noise.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aerodynamic_changes">Aerodynamic changes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737_MAX&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Aerodynamic changes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Boeing_737-8_MAX_Belyakov.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Boeing_737-8_MAX_Belyakov.jpg/220px-Boeing_737-8_MAX_Belyakov.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="330" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Boeing_737-8_MAX_Belyakov.jpg/330px-Boeing_737-8_MAX_Belyakov.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Boeing_737-8_MAX_Belyakov.jpg/440px-Boeing_737-8_MAX_Belyakov.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1067" data-file-height="1600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Boeing_737-8_MAX_Belyakov.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Boeing\'s new "split tip" <a href="/wiki/Wingtip_device" title="Wingtip device">winglet</a> on the 737 MAX</div></div></div>\n<p>The split tip <a href="/wiki/Wingtip_device" title="Wingtip device">wingtip device</a> is designed to maximize lift while staying in the same <a href="/wiki/International_Civil_Aviation_Organization#Aerodrome_reference_code" title="International Civil Aviation Organization">ICAO Aerodrome reference code</a> letter C gates as current Boeing 737s. It traces its design to the <a href="/wiki/McDonnell_Douglas_MD-12" title="McDonnell Douglas MD-12">McDonnell Douglas MD-12</a> 1990s twin-deck concept, proposed for similar gate restrictions before the Boeing merger.<sup id="cite_ref-AW2dec2013_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AW2dec2013-103">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> A MAX&#160;8 with 162 passengers on a 3,000&#160;nmi (5,600&#160;km) flight is projected to have a 1.8% better fuel burn than a blended-winglet-equipped aircraft and 1% over 500&#160;nmi (930&#160;km) at Mach 0.79.<sup id="cite_ref-AW2dec2013_103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AW2dec2013-103">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> The new winglet is 9&#160;ft 6&#160;in (2.90&#160;m) in total height.<sup id="cite_ref-AW1702152_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AW1702152-104">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Aviation_Partners_Inc." class="mw-redirect" title="Aviation Partners Inc.">Aviation Partners</a> offers a similar "Split-Tip Scimitar" winglet for previous 737NGs.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> It resembles a three-way hybrid between a <a href="/wiki/Wingtip_device" title="Wingtip device">blended winglet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wingtip_device#Wingtip_fence" title="Wingtip device">wingtip fence</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wingtip_device" title="Wingtip device">raked wingtip</a>.\n</p><p>Other improvements include a re-contoured <a href="/wiki/Empennage" title="Empennage">tail cone</a>, revised <a href="/wiki/Auxiliary_power_unit" title="Auxiliary power unit">auxiliary power unit</a> inlet and exhaust, aft-body <a href="/wiki/Vortex_generator" title="Vortex generator">vortex generators</a> removal, and other small aerodynamic improvements.<sup id="cite_ref-AW12May2017_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AW12May2017-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Structural_and_other_changes">Structural and other changes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737_MAX&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Structural and other changes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The 8&#160;in (20&#160;cm) taller nose-gear strut maintains the same 17-inch (43&#160;cm) ground clearance of previous 737 <a href="/wiki/Nacelle" title="Nacelle">engine nacelles</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-AW12May2017_28-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AW12May2017-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> New struts and nacelles for the heavier engines add bulk, the main <a href="/wiki/Landing_gear" title="Landing gear">landing gear</a> and supporting structure are beefier, and <a href="/wiki/Fuselage" title="Fuselage">fuselage</a> skins are thicker in some places\xe2\x80\x94thus adding 6,500&#160;lb (2,900&#160;kg) to the MAX 8\'s empty aircraft weight.<sup id="cite_ref-AW12May2017_28-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AW12May2017-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> To preserve fuel and payload capacity, its <a href="/wiki/Maximum_takeoff_weight" title="Maximum takeoff weight">maximum takeoff weight</a> is 7,000&#160;lb (3,200&#160;kg) heavier.<sup id="cite_ref-AW12May2017_28-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AW12May2017-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Rockwell_Collins" title="Rockwell Collins">Rockwell Collins</a> was selected to supply four 15.1-inch (380&#160;mm) landscape <a href="/wiki/Liquid-crystal_display" title="Liquid-crystal display">liquid crystal displays (LCD)</a>, as used on the 787, to improve pilots\' situation awareness and efficiency.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> Boeing plans no major modifications for the 737 MAX flight deck, as it wants to maintain commonality with the 737 Next Generation family. Boeing Commercial Airplanes CEO Jim Albaugh said in 2011, that adding more fly-by-wire control systems would be "very minimal".<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> However, the 737 MAX extended spoilers are <a href="/wiki/Fly-by-wire" title="Fly-by-wire">fly-by-wire</a> controlled.<sup id="cite_ref-AW1702152_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AW1702152-104">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Most of the systems are carried from the 737NG to allow for a short differences-training course to upgrade flight crews.<sup id="cite_ref-AW12May2017_28-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AW12May2017-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In addition to the Speed Trim System (STS), the automatic <a href="/wiki/Tailplane" title="Tailplane">stabilizer</a> control system has been enhanced to include MCAS.  Compared to STS, MCAS has greater authority and cannot be disengaged with the aft and forward column cutout switches. The center console stabilizer-trim cutout switches have been re-wired. Unlike previous versions of the 737, the automatic stabilizer trim control functions cannot be turned off while retaining electric trim switches functionality.<sup id="cite_ref-path-to-disaster_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-path-to-disaster-108">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The MCAS system was deemed necessary by Boeing to meet its internal objective of minimizing training requirements for pilots already qualified on the 737NG. MCAS was to automatically mitigate the pitch-up tendency of the new flight geometry due to the engines being located further forward and higher than on previous 737 models.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> During a reassessment of the aircraft in February 2020, both FAA and EASA determined that the stability and stall characteristics of the plane would have been acceptable with or without MCAS.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>As a production standard, the 737 MAX features the Boeing Sky Interior with overhead bins and LED lighting based on the Boeing 787\'s interior.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Engines">Engines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737_MAX&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Engines">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/CFM_International_LEAP" title="CFM International LEAP">CFM International LEAP</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CFM_LEAP-X_(cropped).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/CFM_LEAP-X_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-CFM_LEAP-X_%28cropped%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/CFM_LEAP-X_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-CFM_LEAP-X_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/CFM_LEAP-X_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-CFM_LEAP-X_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2906" data-file-height="2071" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CFM_LEAP-X_(cropped).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>LEAP mockup</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Boeing_737-9_MAX_CFM_LEAP-1B_PAS.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Boeing_737-9_MAX_CFM_LEAP-1B_PAS.jpg/220px-Boeing_737-9_MAX_CFM_LEAP-1B_PAS.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="152" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Boeing_737-9_MAX_CFM_LEAP-1B_PAS.jpg/330px-Boeing_737-9_MAX_CFM_LEAP-1B_PAS.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Boeing_737-9_MAX_CFM_LEAP-1B_PAS.jpg/440px-Boeing_737-9_MAX_CFM_LEAP-1B_PAS.jpg 2x" data-file-width="588" data-file-height="406" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Boeing_737-9_MAX_CFM_LEAP-1B_PAS.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nacelle with chevrons for noise reduction</div></div></div>\n<p>In 2011, the Leap-1B was initially 10\xe2\x80\x9312% more efficient than the previous 156&#160;cm (61&#160;in) <a href="/wiki/CFM56" class="mw-redirect" title="CFM56">CFM56</a>-7B of the 737NG.<sup id="cite_ref-FG-201108302_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FG-201108302-112">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>The 18-blade, woven <a href="/wiki/Carbon-fiber" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbon-fiber">carbon-fiber</a> fan enables a 9:1 <a href="/wiki/Bypass_ratio" title="Bypass ratio">bypass ratio</a> (up from 5.1:1 with the previous 24-blade <a href="/wiki/Titanium" title="Titanium">titanium</a> fan) for a 40% smaller noise footprint.<sup id="cite_ref-AW12May2017_28-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AW12May2017-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> The CFM56 bypass ranges from 5.1:1 to 5.5:1.<sup id="cite_ref-SafranCFM56_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SafranCFM56-113">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> The two-<a href="/wiki/Spool_(aeronautics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Spool (aeronautics)">spool</a> design has a low-pressure section comprising the fan and three booster stages driven by five <a href="/wiki/Axial_turbine" title="Axial turbine">axial turbine</a> stages and a high-pressure section with a 10-stage <a href="/wiki/Axial_compressor" title="Axial compressor">axial compressor</a> driven by a two-stage turbine.<sup id="cite_ref-AW12May2017_28-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AW12May2017-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> The 41:1 <a href="/wiki/Overall_pressure_ratio" title="Overall pressure ratio">overall pressure ratio</a> increased from 28:1, and advanced hot-section materials enabling higher operating temperatures permit a 15% reduction in <a href="/wiki/Thrust_specific_fuel_consumption" class="mw-redirect" title="Thrust specific fuel consumption">thrust-specific fuel consumption</a> (TSFC), along with 20% lower carbon emissions, 50% lower nitrogen-oxide emissions, though each engine weighs 849&#160;lb (385&#160;kg) more at 6,129&#160;lb (2,780&#160;kg).<sup id="cite_ref-AW12May2017_28-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AW12May2017-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In August 2011, Boeing had to choose between 66&#160;in (168&#160;cm) or 68&#160;in (173&#160;cm) fan diameters necessitating landing gear changes to maintain a 17-inch (43&#160;cm) ground clearance beneath the <a href="/wiki/Re-engine" class="mw-redirect" title="Re-engine">new engines</a>; Boeing Commercial Airplanes chief executive officer Jim Albaugh stated "with a bigger fan you get more efficiency because of the bypass ratio [but also] more weight and more drag", with more airframe changes.<sup id="cite_ref-FG31aug2011_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FG31aug2011-114">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In November 2011, Boeing selected the larger fan diameter, necessitating a 6\xe2\x80\x938&#160;in (150\xe2\x80\x93200&#160;mm) longer nose landing gear.<sup id="cite_ref-FG3nov2011_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FG3nov2011-115">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> In May 2012, Boeing further enlarged the fan to 69.4&#160;in (176&#160;cm), paired with a smaller engine core within minor design changes before the mid-2013 final configuration.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The nacelle features chevrons for noise reduction like the 787.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> A new bleed air digital regulator will improve its reliability.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> The new nacelles being larger and more forward possess aerodynamic properties which act to further increase the pitch rate. The larger engine is cantilevered ahead of and slightly above the wing, and the <a href="/wiki/Laminar_flow" title="Laminar flow">laminar flow</a> engine nacelle lipskin is a GKN Aerospace one-piece, <a href="/wiki/Metal_spinning" title="Metal spinning">spun-formed</a> aluminum sheet inspired by the 787.<sup id="cite_ref-AW1702152_104-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AW1702152-104">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Variants">Variants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737_MAX&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Variants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The 737-700, -800 and -900ER, the most widespread versions of the previous 737NG,<sup id="cite_ref-737_O_D_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-737_O_D-121">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> are succeeded by the 737 MAX 7, MAX 8 and MAX 9, respectively<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> (FAA type certificate: <i>737-7</i>, <i>-8</i>, and <i>-9</i><sup id="cite_ref-FAA-TCDS_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAA-TCDS-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>). The 737 MAX 8 entered service in May 2017,<sup id="cite_ref-Flight22May2017_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flight22May2017-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> and the MAX&#160;9 entered service in March 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-Boeing21mar2018_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boeing21mar2018-123">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> The MAX&#160;7 and MAX&#160;200 (a higher-density version of the MAX&#160;8) were expected to enter service in 2019,<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> and the MAX 10 in 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-Flight4oct2017_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flight4oct2017-126">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In February 2018, Boeing forecast that 60\xe2\x80\x9365% of demand for the airliner would be for the 737 MAX 8 variant, 20\xe2\x80\x9325% for the MAX&#160;9 and MAX&#160;10, and 10% for the MAX&#160;7.<sup id="cite_ref-Flight7feb2018_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flight7feb2018-127">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="737_MAX_7">737 MAX 7</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737_MAX&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: 737 MAX 7">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:EGLF_-_Boeing_737_Max_-_N120IS_(43406207022).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/EGLF_-_Boeing_737_Max_-_N120IS_%2843406207022%29.jpg/220px-EGLF_-_Boeing_737_Max_-_N120IS_%2843406207022%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="187" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/EGLF_-_Boeing_737_Max_-_N120IS_%2843406207022%29.jpg/330px-EGLF_-_Boeing_737_Max_-_N120IS_%2843406207022%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/EGLF_-_Boeing_737_Max_-_N120IS_%2843406207022%29.jpg/440px-EGLF_-_Boeing_737_Max_-_N120IS_%2843406207022%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2353" data-file-height="2000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:EGLF_-_Boeing_737_Max_-_N120IS_(43406207022).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 737 MAX 7 at the 2018 <a href="/wiki/Farnborough_Airshow" title="Farnborough Airshow">Farnborough Airshow</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Originally based on the 737-700, Boeing announced the redesign of the MAX&#160;7, derived from the MAX&#160;8, at the July 2016 <a href="/wiki/Farnborough_Air_Show" class="mw-redirect" title="Farnborough Air Show">Farnborough Air Show</a>, accommodating two more seat rows than the 737-700 for 138 seats, up by 12 seats.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FG_Max_7_redesign_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FG_Max_7_redesign-129">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> The redesign uses the 737-8 wing and landing gear; a pair of over-wing exits rather than the single-door configuration; a 46-inch longer aft fuselage and a 30-inch longer forward fuselage; structural re-gauging and strengthening; and systems and interior modifications to accommodate the longer length.<sup id="cite_ref-AIN160711_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AIN160711-130">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> It is to fly 1,000&#160;nmi (1,900&#160;km) farther than the -700 with 18% lower fuel costs per seat. Boeing predicts the MAX&#160;7 to carry 12 more passengers 400&#160;nmi (740&#160;km) farther than A319neo with 7% lower operating costs per seat.<sup id="cite_ref-AvWeek16mar2018_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AvWeek16mar2018-131">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> Boeing plans to improve its range from 3,850&#160;nmi (4,430&#160;mi; 7,130&#160;km) to 3,915&#160;nmi (4,505&#160;mi; 7,251&#160;km) after 2021.<sup id="cite_ref-FG161031_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FG161031-132">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Production on the first 65-foot-long (20&#160;m) wing <a href="/wiki/Spar_(aeronautics)" title="Spar (aeronautics)">spar</a> for the 737-7 began in October 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-Flight4oct2017_126-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flight4oct2017-126">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> Assembly of the first flight-test aircraft began on November 22, 2017<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> and was rolled out of the factory on February 5, 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> The MAX&#160;7 took off for its first flight on March 16, 2018, from the factory in <a href="/wiki/Renton,_Washington" title="Renton, Washington">Renton, Washington</a>, and flew for three hours over Washington state.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> It reached 250&#160;kn (460&#160;km/h) and 25,000&#160;ft (7,600&#160;m), performed a low <a href="/wiki/Final_approach_(aeronautics)" title="Final approach (aeronautics)">approach</a>, systems checks and an inflight engine restart, and landed at Boeing\'s flight test center in <a href="/wiki/Moses_Lake,_Washington" title="Moses Lake, Washington">Moses Lake, Washington</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Entry into service with launch operator Southwest Airlines was expected in January 2019,<sup id="cite_ref-Flight4oct2017_126-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flight4oct2017-126">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> but the airline deferred these orders until 2023\xe2\x80\x932024.<sup id="cite_ref-Flight2Jan2018_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flight2Jan2018-137">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/WestJet" title="WestJet">WestJet</a> also converted its order for MAX 7s, originally due for delivery in 2019, into MAX 8s and is not expected to take any MAX 7s until at least 2021.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> Customers for the aircraft include Southwest Airlines (30), WestJet (22) and <a href="/wiki/ILFC" class="mw-redirect" title="ILFC">ILFC</a> Aviation (5).<sup id="cite_ref-737_O_D_121-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-737_O_D-121">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> As of March 2018, the MAX 7 had fewer than 100 orders among over 4,300 total sales of the MAX.<sup id="cite_ref-AvWeek16mar2018_131-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AvWeek16mar2018-131">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> In early 2021, Boeing announced that the first 737 MAX 7 deliveries are expected later in the year.<sup id="cite_ref-Flight2feb2021_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flight2feb2021-139">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Southwest_Airlines" title="Southwest Airlines">Southwest</a> committed to order 100 MAX 7 aircraft with deliveries to begin in 2022;<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> the airline had previously considered the <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A220" title="Airbus A220">Airbus A220</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="737_MAX_8">737 MAX 8</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737_MAX&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: 737 MAX 8">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Boeing_737-8_MAX_FIA16_Belyakov_(cropped).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Boeing_737-8_MAX_FIA16_Belyakov_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-Boeing_737-8_MAX_FIA16_Belyakov_%28cropped%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Boeing_737-8_MAX_FIA16_Belyakov_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-Boeing_737-8_MAX_FIA16_Belyakov_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Boeing_737-8_MAX_FIA16_Belyakov_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-Boeing_737-8_MAX_FIA16_Belyakov_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="683" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Boeing_737-8_MAX_FIA16_Belyakov_(cropped).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A top view of the 737 MAX 8 showing double <a href="/wiki/Overwing_exits" title="Overwing exits">overwing exits</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The first variant developed in the 737 MAX series, the MAX&#160;8 replaces the <a href="/wiki/737-800" class="mw-redirect" title="737-800">737-800</a> with a longer fuselage than the MAX&#160;7. Boeing plans to improve its range from 3,515&#160;nmi (4,045&#160;mi; 6,510&#160;km) to 3,610&#160;nmi (4,150&#160;mi; 6,690&#160;km) after 2021.<sup id="cite_ref-FG161031_132-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FG161031-132">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> On July 23, 2013, Boeing completed the firm configuration for the 737 MAX 8.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> The MAX&#160;8 has a lower empty weight and higher maximum takeoff weight than the A320neo. During a test flight conducted for <i>Aviation Week</i>, while cruising at a true airspeed of 449&#160;kn (832&#160;km/h) and a weight of 140,500&#160;lb (63,700&#160;kg), at a lower than optimal altitude (FL350 vs. the preferred FL390) and with an "unusually far forward" <a href="/wiki/Center_of_gravity" class="mw-redirect" title="Center of gravity">center of gravity</a>, the test aircraft consumed 4,460&#160;lb (2,020&#160;kg) of fuel per hour.<sup id="cite_ref-AW12May2017_28-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AW12May2017-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Boeing 737 MAX 8 completed its first flight test in <a href="/wiki/La_Paz" title="La Paz">La Paz</a>, Bolivia. The 13,300-foot altitude at <a href="/wiki/El_Alto_International_Airport" title="El Alto International Airport">El Alto International Airport</a> tested the MAX\'s capability to take off and land at high altitudes.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> Its first commercial flight was operated by <a href="/wiki/Malindo_Air" title="Malindo Air">Malindo Air</a> on May 22, 2017, between Kuala Lumpur and Singapore as Flight OD803.<sup id="cite_ref-Flight22May2017_2-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flight22May2017-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>\nIn early 2017, a new -8 was valued at $52.85<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>million, rising to below $54.5<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>million by mid<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>2018.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="737_MAX_200">737 MAX 200</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737_MAX&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: 737 MAX 200">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>In September 2014, Boeing launched a high-density version of the 737 MAX 8, the 737 MAX 200, named for seating for up to 200 passengers in a single-class high-density configuration with <a href="/wiki/Airline_seat#&quot;Slimline&quot;_economy_seating" title="Airline seat">slimline seats</a>; an extra pair of <a href="/wiki/Emergency_exit#Aircraft" title="Emergency exit">exit doors</a> is required because of the higher passenger capacity. Boeing states that this version would be 20% more cost-efficient per seat than current 737 models, and would be the most efficient narrow-body on the market when delivered, including 5% lower operating costs than the 737 MAX 8.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> Three of eight galley trolleys are removed to accommodate more passenger space.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> An order with <a href="/wiki/Ryanair" title="Ryanair">Ryanair</a> for 100 aircraft was finalized in December 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> The variant is designated <i>737-8200</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In mid-November 2018, the first of the 135 ordered by Ryanair rolled out, in a 197-seat configuration.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> It was first flown from Renton on January 13, 2019,<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> and was due to enter service in April 2019, with another four MAX 200s expected later in 2019,<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> though these deliveries were deferred while the MAX was grounded; Ryanair has stated that it intends to place more orders once flights resume.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> In November 2019, Ryanair informed its pilots that, due to an unspecified design issue with the additional over-wing exit doors, it did not expect to receive any MAX 200s until late April or early May 2020, with "at best" ten aircraft in service for the peak summer season.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Besides Ryanair, <a href="/wiki/VietJet_Air" title="VietJet Air">VietJet Air</a> is also one of the few customers of the MAX 200 variant with an order of 100 airplanes placed in May 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> Similar to Ryanair, VietJet has chosen to designate its aircraft as <i>737-8</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On December 3, 2020, Ryanair ordered 75 MAX 8-200s, increasing its order book to 210 aircraft, the first large order since the grounding, for a list value of $7&#8201;bn, while the true value is estimated at $3&#8201;bn or less.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup>\nThe high-density variant was certificated by the FAA on 31 March 2021.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Proposed_737-8ERX">Proposed 737-8ERX</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737_MAX&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Proposed 737-8ERX">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Airlines have been shown a 737-8ERX concept based on the 737 MAX 8 with a higher 194,700&#160;lb (88.3&#160;t) maximum take-off weight using wings, landing gear, and central section from the MAX&#160;9 to provide a longer range of 4,000 nautical miles (4,600&#160;mi; 7,400&#160;km) with seating for 150, closer to the <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A320neo_family" title="Airbus A320neo family">Airbus A321LR</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="737_MAX_9">737 MAX 9</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Boeing_737_MAX&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: 737 MAX 9">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Boeing,_N7379E,_Boeing_737-9_MAX_(cropped).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Boeing%2C_N7379E%2C_Boeing_737-9_MAX_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-Boeing%2C_N7379E%2C_Boeing_737-9_MAX_%28cropped%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Boeing%2C_N7379E%2C_Boeing_737-9_MAX_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-Boeing%2C_N7379E%2C_Boeing_737-9_MAX_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Boeing%2C_N7379E%2C_Boeing_737-9_MAX_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-Boeing%2C_N7379E%2C_Boeing_737-9_MAX_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2104" data-file-height="1446" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Boeing,_N7379E,_Boeing_737-9_MAX_(cropped).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>737 MAX 9 first flight on April 13, 2017</div></div></div>\n<p>The 737 MAX 9 will replace the 737-900 and has a longer fuselage than the MAX&#160;8. Boeing plans to improve its range from 3,510&#160;nmi (4,040&#160;mi; 6,500&#160;km) to 3,605&#160;nmi (4,149&#160;mi; 6,676&#160;km) after 2021.<sup id="cite_ref-FG161031_132-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FG161031-132">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> Lion Air was the launch customer with an order for 201 in February 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-AW170215_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AW170215-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> It made its roll-out on March 7, 2017, and first flight on April 13, 2017;<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> it took off from Renton Municipal Airport and landed at <a href="/wiki/Boeing_Field" title="Boeing Field">Boeing Field</a> after a 2 hr 42 min flight.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> It was presented at the 2017 Paris Air Show.<sup id="cite_ref-AvWeek9Jun2017_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AvWeek9Jun2017-162">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Boeing 737-9 flight tests were scheduled to run through 2017, with 30% of the <span class="nowrap">-8</span> tests repeated; aircraft 1D001 was used for auto-land, avionics, flutter, and mostly stability-and-control trials, while 1D002 was used for environment control system testing.<sup id="cite_ref-AIN8Nov2017_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AIN8Nov2017-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> It was certified by February 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> Asian low-cost carrier Lion Air Group took delivery of the first on March 21, 2018, before entering service with <a href="/wiki/Thai_Lion_Air" title="Thai Lion Air">Thai Lion Air</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Boeing21mar2018_123-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boeing21mar2018-123">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> As the competing <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A320neo_family" title="Airbus A320neo
